Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Conditions for the Excuse of Istihadah and Types of Wudu
Explaining conditions for initial and continued excuse, types of wudu and cessation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the initial establishment and continuance of the excuse in istihadah, and distinguish between complete and incomplete wudu and cessation, applying the rulings to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
A slight interruption in bleeding does not break the initial excuse; complete cessation for an entire prayer time ends it.

In this lesson we delve into the Hanafi rules for a woman experiencing chronic non-menstrual bleeding. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the precise conditions for becoming and remaining a ma'dhurah, and the implications for her wudu and prayers. We will also explore the concepts of complete and incomplete wudu and cessation, which determine when she must repeat a prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
Distinguish between initial establishment of the excuse and its continuance/removal. The conditions differ.
Text: 'قيدنا بكونه شرط البقاء لأن شرط ثبوته ابتداء بأن يستوعب وقتاً كاملاً.'
2
Understand the majority initial condition
Most books require bleeding to cover the entire prayer time, but a slight interruption is overlooked (considered non-existent).
Fath al-Qadir reconciles: 'الانقطاع اليسير ملحق بالعدم'.
3
Consider the differing view from al-Kafi
Al-Kafi says the condition is that she cannot find a clean moment to perform wudu. This is reconciled as an interpretation.
Text: 'وفي فتح القدير أن ما في الكافي يصلح تفسيراً لما في غيره'.
4
Distinguish types of wudu and cessation
Complete wudu: done while blood is stopped. Incomplete: done while flowing. Complete cessation: stops full time. Incomplete: less than full time.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj explains the consequences.
5
Apply to the Zuhr/Asr scenario
Walk through the example: flowing wudu, then stop before prayer, cessation continues until time exit: wudu invalid, must repeat. For Asr: stopped wudu, continues: valid.
This illustrates the practical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهذا إذا لم يمض عليهم وقت فرض إلا وذلك الحدث يوجد فيه) أي وحكم الاستحاضة والعذر يبقى إذا لم يمض على أصحابهما وقت صلاة إلا والحدث الذي ابتليت به يوجد فيه ولو قليلاً حتى لو انقطع وقتاً كاملاً خرج عن كونه عذراً. قيدنا بكونه شرط البقاء لأن شرط ثبوته ابتداء بأن يستوعب وقتاً كاملاً. كذا في أكثر الكتب. وفي النهاية يشترط في الابتداء دوام السيلان من أول الوقت إلى آخره اعتباراً بالسقوط فإنه لا يتم حتى ينقطع في الوقت كله. وفي شرح الشيخ حميد الدين الضرير فالشرط في الابتداء أن يكون الحدث جميع الوقت حتى لو لم يستغرق كل الوقت لا تكون مستحاضة، وظاهره أنه لو انقطع في الوقت زمناً يسيراً لا تكون مستحاضة. وفي الكافي ما يخالفه فإنه قال : إنما يصير صاحب عذر إذا لم يجد في وقت صلاة زماناً يتوضأ فيه خالياً عن الحدث. وفي التبيين أن الأظهر خلاف ما في الكافي. وفي فتح القدير أن ما في الكافي يصلح تفسيراً لما في غيره إذ قل ما يستمر كمال وقت بحيث لا ينقطع لحظة فيؤدّي إلى نفي تحققه إلا في الإمكان بخلاف جانب الصحة منه فإنه يدوم انقطاعه وقتاً كاملاً وهو مما يتحقق اهـ. وفي شرح الدرر والغرر لمنلاخسرو : لا مخالفة بين ما في عامة الكتب وما ذكره في الكافي بدليل أن شراح الجامع الخلاطي قالوا في شرح قوله لأن زوال العذر يثبت باستيعاب الوقت كالثبوت أن الانقطاع الكامل معتبر في إبطال رخصة المعذور والقصر غير معتبر إجماعاً فاحتيج إلى حد فاصل، فقدرنا بوقت الصلاة كما قدرنا به ثبوت العذر ابتداء فإنه يشترط لثبوته ابتداء دوام السيلان قال في شرح التنوير : والمعذور إنما تبقى طهارته في الوقت بشرطين : إذا توضأ لعذره ولم يطرأ عليه حدث آخر، أما إذا توضأ الحدث آخر وعذره منقطع ثم سال أو توضأ لعذره ثم طرأ عليه حدث آخر فلا تبقى طهارته .اهـ. فإنه صريح في أن السيلان بدون خروج الوقت مبطل وليس كذلك لما علمت من صريح النقل فتنبه . ثم رأيت في القهستاني أيضاً ما هو صريح في ذلك حيث قال: لو استحيضت فدخل وقت العصر والدم منقطع فتوضأت وصلت العصر ثم سال الدم في هذا الوقت لم ينتقض وضوءها اهـ. ثم رأيت بعد حين ما يرفع الإشكال ويوضح الحال وهو أن صاحب المنية قد السيلان من أول الوقت إلى أخره لأنه إنما يصير صاحب عذر ابتداء فإنه يشترط لثبوته ابتداء دوام أول الوقت إلى آخره لأنه إنما يصير صاحب عذر ابتداء إذا لم يجد في وقت صلاة من زماناً يتوضأ فيه ويصلي خالياً عن الحدث الذي ابتلى به .اهـ. فالحاصل أن صاحب العذر ابتداء من استوعب عذره تمام وقت صلاة ولو حكماً لأن الانقطاع اليسير ملحق بالعدم وفي البقاء من وجد عذره في جزء من الوقت وفي الزوال يشترط استيعاب الانقطاع حقيقة. وفي السراج الوهاج للمستحاضة وضو آن كامل وناقص، فالكامل أن تتوضأ والدم منقطع فهذه لا يضرها خروج الوقت إذا لم يسل إلى خروجه والناقص أن تتوضأ وهو سائل فهذه يضرها خروجه، سال بعد ذلك أولاً. ولها انقطاعان كامل وناقص فالكامل أن ينقطع وقتاً كاملاً فهذا يوجب الزوال ويمنع اتصال الدم الثاني بالأول، والناقص أن ينقطع دونه فهذا لا يزيله ويكون ما بعده كدم متصل وبيانه إذا زالت الشمس ودمها سائل فتوضأت على السيلان ثم انقطع قبل الشروع في صلاة الظهر أو بعده قبل القعود قدر التشهد أو بعده قبل السلام عند الإمام ودام الانقطاع حتى خرج وقت الظهر انتقض وضوءها لأنه ناقص فأفسده خروج الوقت، ثم إذا توضأت للعصر فتم الانقطاع حتى غربت الشمس لم ينتقض وضوءها لأنه كامل فلا يضره الخروج، ولكن عليها إعادة الظهر لأن دمها انقطع وقتاً كاملاً وتبين أنها صلت الظهر بطهارة العذر والعذر زائل، ولا يجب عليها إعادة العصر لأن فساد الظهر إنما عرف بعد الغروب. وأما إذا كان دمها انقطع بعدما فرغت من صلاة الظهر أو بعد القعود قدر التشهد على قولهما فإنها لا تعيد الظهر لأن عذرها زال بعد الفراغ كالمتيمم إذا رأى الماء بعد الفراغ من الصلاة اهـ. وظن القوام الأتقاني في غاية البيان أن ما ذكر في المتن تعريف للمستحاضة فأورد عليه الحائض والنفساء لأن الحائض قد تكون بهذه المثابة بأن لا عليها وقت إلا وهو يوجد فيه، واختار تعريفاً للمستحاضة بأنها هي التي ترى الدم مستغرقاً وقت صلاة في الابتداء من غير شرط استمرار في البقاء في زمان لا يعتبر من الحيض والنفاس .اهـ. وليس كما ظن بل هو شرط لها لا تعريف وقد قدمنا تعريف الاستحاضة.
He said: (And this is if no time of a fard passes upon them except that the hadath exists in it) i.e. the ruling of istihadah and the excuse remains if no prayer time passes upon its people except that the hadath with which they are afflicted exists in it, even if little, until if it stops for a complete time, they leave the state of excuse. We restricted it as a condition for permanence, because the condition for its initial establishment is that it cover a complete time. Thus in most books. And in al-Nihaya, it requires at the beginning the continuance of flow from the beginning of the time to its end, considering the cessation, for it is not complete until it stops in the entire time. And in the commentary of Shaykh Hamid al-Din al-Darir, the condition at the beginning is that the hadath be in all the time, so that if it does not cover all the time, she is not a mustahadah. Apparently, if it stops for a short time within the time, she is not a mustahadah. And in al-Kafi is something contrary: he said, a person only becomes a sahib 'udhr if he does not find in a prayer time a period to perform wudu free from the hadath. And in al-Tabyin, the apparent is contrary to al-Kafi. And in Fath al-Qadir, what is in al-Kafi can serve as an interpretation of what is in others, for rarely does a full time continue without stopping for a moment, leading to denial of its actuality except in possibility, unlike the side of health where cessation continues for a full time, which is actual. In Sharh al-Durar wa al-Ghurar by Mulla Khusraw: no contradiction between what is in the general books and what is in al-Kafi, as the commentators of al-Jami' al-Khilati said in explanation of his statement: 'because the removal of the excuse is established by covering the time just as the establishment (is), that complete cessation is considered in invalidating the license of the excused person, and short is not considered by consensus.' So a dividing limit is needed, so we determined it by the prayer time just as we determined by it the initial establishment of the excuse, for its initial establishment requires the continuance of flow. He said in Sharh al-Tanwir: The excused person only retains his purity in the time with two conditions: if he performed wudu for his excuse and no other hadath occurs to him. But if he performed wudu for another hadath while his excuse is stopped then it flowed, or he performed wudu for his excuse then another hadath occurred, his purity does not remain. This is explicit that flowing without exit of time invalidates, but it is not so as you know from explicit transmission, so be careful. Then I saw in al-Qahustani also what is explicit in that: he said: if a woman is mustahadah and the time of Asr enters and the blood is stopped, so she performs wudu and prays Asr, then blood flows in this time, her wudu is not invalidated. Then later I saw what removes the problem and clarifies the situation: that the author of al-Munyah has the flow from the beginning of the time to its end, because he only becomes a sahib 'udhr initially, for its initial establishment requires the continuance of flow from the beginning of the time to its end, because he only becomes a sahib 'udhr initially if he does not find in a prayer time a period to perform wudu and pray free from the hadath with which he is afflicted. So the summary: the sahib 'udhr initially is one whose excuse covers an entire prayer time, even legally, because a slight interruption is considered non-existent. And in permanence, one whose excuse is found in part of the time. And in removal, complete cessation is required in reality. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, the mustahadah has two wudus: complete and incomplete. The complete is that she performs wudu while blood is stopped; this one is not harmed by the exit of time if it does not flow until its exit. The incomplete is that she performs wudu while it is flowing; this one is harmed by its exit, regardless of whether it flowed afterwards. And she has two cessations: complete and incomplete. The complete is that it stops for a complete time; this causes removal and prevents connection of the second blood with the first. The incomplete is that it stops less than that; this does not remove it, and what follows is like connected blood. Its explanation: if the sun has passed the meridian and her blood is flowing, then she performs wudu while it is flowing, then it stops before starting the Zuhr prayer or after it before sitting for the amount of tashahhud, or after that before the salam according to Imam, and the cessation continues until Zuhr time exits, her wudu is invalidated because it is incomplete, so the exit of time spoils it. Then if she performs wudu for Asr and the cessation is complete until the sun sets, her wudu is not invalidated because it is complete, so the exit does not harm her, but she must repeat Zuhr because her blood stopped for a complete time and it becomes clear that she prayed Zuhr with the purity of excuse while the excuse had ceased. She is not required to repeat Asr because the invalidity of Zuhr was only known after sunset. If her blood stopped after she finished the Zuhr prayer or after sitting for the amount of tashahhud according to the two, then she does not repeat Zuhr because her excuse ceased after completion, like the tayammum person who sees water after completing prayer. And al-Qawam al-Aqani in Ghayat al-Bayan thought that what was mentioned in the text was a definition of mustahadah, so he objected against it the menstruating and postpartum woman because they may be in such a state. He chose a definition: mustahadah is she who sees blood covering a prayer time initially without condition of continuance in permanence in a period not considered from hayd or nifas. But it is not as he thought; rather it is a condition for her, not a definition, and we have already presented the definition of istihadah.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the conditions for being a sahib al-'udhr in istihadah. It explains that the excuse remains only if no complete prayer time passes without the blood appearing (even a little). For the initial establishment of the excuse, most books require the blood to cover the entire time, with a slight interruption overlooked. The commentary reconciles differing views: al-Kafi's condition is interpreted leniently. The discussion then moves to types of wudu and cessation: complete (blood stopped) and incomplete (flowing). An example: if at Zuhr time blood is flowing, she does wudu while flowing, then it stops before praying Zuhr, then continues after Zuhr has exited—her wudu breaks. But if for Asr she does wudu while stopped and it remains stopped until sunset, her wudu remains valid, but she must repeat Zuhr because the excuse had actually ended (blood stopped for complete time). The passage ends with a critique by al-Qawam al-Aqani who mistook the condition for a definition.

Key Terms
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Non-menstrual vaginal bleeding continuing beyond normal period, making woman a ma'dhurah
صاحب العذر (sahib al-'udhr): Person with chronic excuse preventing continuous purity
الانقطاع الكامل (al-inqita' al-kamil): Complete cessation of bleeding for an entire prayer time
وضوء كامل (wudu kamil): Wudu performed while blood is not flowing (complete purity)
وضوء ناقص (wudu naqis): Wudu performed while blood is flowing (incomplete purity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for the initial establishment of the excuse of istihadah according to most Hanafi books?
A) Bleeding must be continuous without any interruption
B) Bleeding must cover the entire prayer time, but a slight interruption is excused
C) The woman must not find any time to perform wudu without bleeding
D) Bleeding must occur only during the night

Key Takeaway
The excuse of istihadah is established when bleeding covers a full prayer time (even legally), continues as long as bleeding occurs in part of each time, and ends only with complete cessation for an entire time.

Definition of Nifas (Postpartum Bleeding)
Defining nifas linguistically and legally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of nifas, explain its linguistic derivation, and identify the Hanafi position on the absence of a fixed maximum duration.

Core Idea
Nifas is post-childbirth blood; Hanafi school holds no fixed maximum, unlike other schools.

In this lesson, we unpack the definition of nifas from al-Bahr al-Raiq. We explore both the linguistic roots and the legal application, and examine an additional note on how wudu performed for a specific excuse remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Find the core phrase 'النفاس دم يعقب الولد شرعاً' which defines nifas as blood that follows childbirth legally.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
Note that 'نفاس' is the verbal noun of 'نفس' (to give birth), and the name is connected to the soul (nafs) which is sustained by blood.
The text rejects alternative derivations from breathing or exit of child.
3
Recognize the legal implication
Hanafi school does not fix a maximum duration; instead, it relies on customary practice ('urf). This contrasts with other schools.
Cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'i max 60 days, Hanbali 40 days.
4
Examine the wudu note
The passage adds that wudu performed due to an excuse (like nifas) is not invalidated by the occurrence of that excuse itself, only by what it was performed for.
This reinforces that a woman in nifas can perform wudu and pray validly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمضي قوله : (والنفاس دم يعقب الولد شرعاً. وفي اللغة هو مصدر نفست المرأة بضم النون وفتحها إذا ولدت فهي نفساء وهن نفاس. وإنما سمى الدم به لأن النفس التي هي اسم لجملة الحيوان قوامها بالدم، وقولهم النفاس هو الدم الخارج عقيب الولد تسمية بالمصدر كالحيض. فأما اشتقاقه من تنفس الرحم أو خروج النفس بمعنى الولد فليس بذاك. كذا في المغرب. وأفاد بما قاله الحصكفي وعزاه إلى أحكام الفقه، وعلله شراحها المحقق الحلبي بقوله : لأن الوضوء لم صرح لذلك العذر حتى لا ينتقض به بل وقع لغيره وإنما ينتقض به ما وقع له اهـ. فأفاد تخصيص يقع العبارات السابقة بما إذا كان الوضوء من العذر الذي ابتلي به لا من غيره فالحمد لله تعالى على ما
He said: (And nifas is blood that follows the birth legally. And in language, it is the masdar of nafasat al-mar'atu with dam or fath of nun when she gives birth; she is nufasa', and they are nifaas. It is called this because the soul (nafs) which is the name for the living being is sustained by blood. And their saying: nifas is blood that exits after birth is a verbal noun like hayd. As for its derivation from 'breathing of the womb' or 'exit of the soul' meaning the child, that is not correct. This is from al-Maghrib. And he says: what al-Haskafi said and attributed to Ahkam al-Fiqh, and its commentators, al-Muhaqqiq al-Halabi, reasoned: 'Because the wudu was stated for that excuse so that it is not invalidated by it, rather it occurred for another, and only what it occurred for invalidates it.' So it gives a specification to the previous expressions that it is when the wudu is for the excuse that one is afflicted with, not for another. So praise be to Allah for that.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage defines nifas (postpartum bleeding). Legally, nifas is the blood that follows childbirth. Linguistically, the word nifas is the verbal noun of 'nafasat' when a woman gives birth. The blood is called nifas because the soul (nafs) is sustained by blood. The author rejects alternate derivations (breathing of womb or exit of child). The passage then adds a note from al-Haskafi: the wudu performed for the excuse is specifically not invalidated by the excuse's own occurrence; rather, it is invalidated by what it was performed for. This applies only when the wudu is done for the specific excuse, not for other reasons.

Key Terms
النفاس (al-nifas): Blood that legally follows childbirth
الولد (al-walad): Child, birth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum duration of nifas?
A) 40 days
B) 60 days
C) No fixed maximum
D) 30 days

Key Takeaway
Nifas is blood following childbirth; the Hanafi school does not fix a maximum duration, relying on custom, while other schools set limits.

Taharah — Purification

Conditions for Being a Sahib 'Udhr and the Mustahada's Wudu
Explains conditions for chronic excuse and mustahada's wudu types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the initial condition for being a sahib 'udhr, distinguish between complete and incomplete wudu for a mustahada, and apply the rule that the exit of prayer time nullifies only the incomplete wudu.

Core Idea
A mustahada's wudu is nullified by time exit only if performed while bleeding; if performed while blood stops, it remains valid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the foundational rule for anyone with a chronic excuse: the excuse must encompass the entire prayer time. For the mustahada (a woman with chronic non-menstrual bleeding), this determines whether her wudu is considered 'complete' or 'incomplete' and whether it is nullified when the prayer time ends. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the validity of her prayers and for distinguishing between the rulings for the mustahada and other excused persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for initial excuse
The first part states that the excuse (blood flow) must be present from the beginning to the end of the prayer time for a person to be considered a sahib 'udhr initially. A slight interruption is disregarded.
This is a condition, not a definition of mustahada.
2
Distinguish complete vs incomplete wudu
Complete wudu is performed when the blood has stopped; it is not nullified by the exit of the prayer time if the bleeding does not resume. Incomplete wudu is performed while bleeding; it is nullified when the prayer time exits.
The type of wudu depends on the state of bleeding at the time of wudu.
3
Understand the two types of interruption
A complete interruption means the blood stops for an entire prayer time, causing the excuse to cease and preventing the second bleeding from connecting to the first. An incomplete interruption is less than that, and the excuse continues.
A complete interruption retroactively reveals that the excuse had ceased during the previous time.
4
Apply the example from the text
Follow the example: a mustahada performs wudu for Zuhr while bleeding, then blood stops at various points. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the blood stops before sitting for tashahhud, the wudu is nullified by time exit; if after, it is not. There is an intra-Hanafi difference. She must repeat Zuhr if the blood stops for the entire Asr time, proving her excuse had ceased during Zuhr.
The repetition of Zuhr is due to the cessation of the excuse, not the nullification of wudu.

The flow from the beginning of the time to its end, because he only becomes a person with an excuse initially, so it is required for its initial establishment the continuity from the beginning of the time to its end, because he only becomes a person with an excuse initially if he does not find in a prayer time a time in which to perform wudu and pray free from the impurity that afflicts him. End. So the conclusion is that a person with an excuse initially is one whose excuse encompasses the entire prayer time, even constructively, because a slight interruption is considered nonexistent. And for one who persists (i.e., who has the excuse intermittently), it suffices that he finds his excuse in a part of the time. And for cessation, it is required that the interruption actually encompasses the entire time. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: For the mustahada, there is complete and incomplete wudu. The complete is that she performs wudu while the blood is stopped, so the exit of the time does not harm her if it does not flow until its exit. The incomplete is that she performs wudu while it is flowing, so the exit of the time harms her, whether it flows afterwards or not. She has two types of interruption: complete and incomplete. The complete is that it stops for a complete time, causing cessation and preventing the connection of the second blood to the first. The incomplete is that it stops for less than that, not removing it, and what follows is like continuous blood. An explanation: When the sun passed its zenith and her blood was flowing, she performed wudu while flowing, then it stopped before starting Zuhr prayer, or after starting but before sitting for the amount of tashahhud, or after that but before taslim according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and the interruption continued until the time of Zuhr exited: her wudu is nullified because it was incomplete, so the exit of time invalidated it. Then if she performed wudu for Asr and the interruption continued until sunset, her wudu is not nullified because it was complete, so exit does not harm her. However, she must repeat Zuhr because her blood stopped for a complete time and it becomes clear that she prayed Zuhr with the purity of an excuse while the excuse had ceased. She is not required to repeat Asr because the invalidity of Zuhr was only known after sunset. As for if her blood stopped after she finished Zuhr prayer, or after sitting for the amount of tashahhud according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she does not repeat Zuhr because her excuse ceased after completion, like a mutayammim who sees water after completing prayer. End. And al-Qawwam al-Atqani in Ghayat al-Bayan thought that what was mentioned in the text was a definition of the mustahada, so he objected that the menstruating woman and the woman in nifas could be in such a situation, because the menstruating woman may have no time except that the blood is present in it. He chose a definition of the mustahada as she who sees blood encompassing a prayer time initially without the condition of continuity in duration in a time considered not from menstruation or nifas. End. But it is not as he thought; rather it is a condition for her, not a definition, and we have already presented the definition of istihadah.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the conditions for being considered a sahib 'udhr (a person with a chronic excuse) and the rules for a mustahada's wudu. The author begins by stating the condition for initial establishment of the excuse: the blood must flow continuously from the beginning to the end of the prayer time. If there is a slight interruption, it is disregarded. Then he distinguishes between a person who has the excuse initially and one who has it intermittently. For the mustahada, there are two types of wudu: complete (when she performs wudu while the bleeding has stopped) and incomplete (when she performs wudu while bleeding). The complete wudu is not nullified by the exit of the prayer time if the bleeding does not resume before the end of the time. The incomplete wudu is nullified when the time exits. Similarly, she has two types of interruption: complete (stops for a whole prayer time) and incomplete (stops for less). The complete interruption causes the excuse to cease and prevents the second blood from connecting to the first. The incomplete interruption does not cause cessation. An example is given: a woman performs wudu for Zuhr while bleeding, then it stops before she starts prayer or after starting but before sitting for tashahhud or after that but before taslim according to Imam Abu Hanifa. If the interruption continues until the time of Zuhr exits, her wudu is invalidated. Then for Asr, if she performs wudu and the bleeding stops completely until sunset, her wudu remains valid. However, she must repeat Zuhr because the bleeding stopped for a complete time, showing that her excuse had ceased. She does not repeat Asr because the invalidation of Zuhr was only known after sunset. Finally, the author refutes a misunderstanding by al-Qawwam al-Atqani, clarifying that the text is giving a condition for being a sahib 'udhr, not a definition of mustahada.

Key Terms
صاحب عذر (sahib 'udhr): A person with a chronic excuse that prevents them from maintaining wudu for an entire prayer time, such as a mustahada.
مستحاضة (mustahada): A woman who experiences non-menstrual bleeding (istihadah) that continues beyond her normal period or in a pattern not conforming to menstruation.
وضوء كامل (wudu kamil): Complete wudu performed by a mustahada when the bleeding has stopped, which is not nullified by the exit of the prayer time if the bleeding does not resume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the bleeding stops
B) When she exits the mosque
C) When the time of the prayer exits
D) When she performs another wudu

Key Takeaway
A mustahada's wudu is either complete (if blood stops) or incomplete (if flowing); only incomplete wudu is nullified by the exit of prayer time, and if the blood stops for a full prayer time, the excuse ceases retroactively.

Definition of Nifas: Linguistic and Legal
Provides linguistic derivation and legal definition of nifas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definition of nifas according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Nifas is legally blood following childbirth, derived linguistically from the masdar meaning childbirth.

This passage clarifies what nifas (postnatal bleeding) means both linguistically and in Islamic law. Understanding the definition is essential because it determines a woman's state of purity and the associated prohibitions. The text provides a precise lexical derivation and refutes incorrect etymologies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Find the exact technical definition: nifas is blood that follows childbirth.
Legal definition: 'دم يعقب الولد شرعاً'.
2
Note the linguistic origin
Recognize that 'nifas' is the masdar of 'nafasat' (to give birth). The woman is called 'nufasa''.
Linguistic root: the verb نَفِسَت or نَفَسَت.
3
Understand the naming rationale
The blood is called nifas because the soul (nafs) depends on blood; it is naming the substance by the infinitive.
Analogy with 'hayd' (menstruation).
4
Reject invalid derivations
Two false etymologies are refuted: derivation from 'tanafus al-rahim' (breathing of womb) or from 'nafs' meaning the child.
The correct derivation is from childbirth, not breathing.
5
Apply the definition
Relate this definition to fiqh: nifas affects rulings in taharah, prayer, fasting, and sexual intercourse.
The definition is foundational for subsequent rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والنفاس دم يعقب الولد شرعاً. وفي اللغة هو مصدر نفست المرأة بضم النون وفتحها إذا ولدت فهي نفساء وهن نفاس. وإنما سمي الدم به لأن النفس التي هي اسم لجملة الحيوان قوامها بالدم، وقولهم النفاس هو الدم الخارج عقيب الولد تسمية بالمصدر كالحيض. فأما اشتقاقه من تنفس الرحم أو خروج النفس بمعنى الولد فليس بذاك. كذا في المغرب.
His saying: (And nifas is blood that follows the child legally. Linguistically, it is the masdar of 'nafasat al-mar'ah' (the woman gave birth) with damm on the noon and fatha on it. When she gives birth, she is nufasa', and they are nifas. It is only called blood by it because the 'nafs' (soul), which is the name for the entire living being, is sustained by blood. And their saying that nifas is the blood exiting after the child is a naming of the substance by the infinitive like hayd. As for its derivation from 'tanafus al-rahim' (the breathing of the womb) or the exit of 'nafs' meaning the child, that is not valid. Thus in al-Mughrib.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling that clarifies the meaning of nifas (postnatal bleeding). The author first gives the legal definition: blood that follows childbirth. Then he explains the linguistic origin: 'nifas' is the masdar (verbal noun) of the verb 'nafasat' meaning to give birth. The woman is called 'nufasa''. The reason the blood is called nifas is that the soul (nafs) depends on blood. It is a case of naming the substance (the blood) by the infinitive (the action of giving birth), similar to how menstruation is called 'hayd'. He refutes two other proposed etymologies: derivation from 'breathing of the womb' or from 'exit of the soul (child)'. The source is given as al-Mughrib.

Key Terms
نفاس (nifas): Blood that exits after childbirth, legally considered to make the woman in a state similar to menstruation.
نفساء (nufasa'): A woman in the state of nifas, i.e., experiencing postnatal bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Bleeding that occurs during pregnancy
B) Blood that follows childbirth
C) Blood that comes after menstruation
D) Blood from a wound

Key Takeaway
Nifas is legally defined as blood after childbirth, not derived from breathing or the soul.

Childbirth Without Blood: Ghusl Obligation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nifas definition and ghusl obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on ghusl for a woman who gives birth without blood, explain the thamarah al-khilaf, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Childbirth without blood: precaution versus strict evidence – Abu Hanifa's view prevails as the operative fatwa.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about a woman who gives birth but sees no blood. Two towering imams, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, differed on whether she is nufasa' and obligated to perform ghusl. Understanding their reasoning and the later tarjih will sharpen your ability to appreciate how precaution (ihtiyat) and textual adherence interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What exactly is the legal question? It is whether the absence of visible blood after childbirth negates the status of nifas and the obligation of ghusl.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds ghusl as wajib and considers her nufasa' because childbirth normally involves at least some blood. As a precaution, the fast is invalidated.
Precautionary principle: the default is that nifas occurs unless proven otherwise.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his dalil
Abu Yusuf says ghusl is not obligatory because the obligation is tied to the existence of nifas blood, and it is absent. The fast remains valid.
Strict textual link: nifas is defined by blood exiting the womb.
4
Examine the later tarjih
Al-Zayla'i preferred Abu Yusuf, but Sadr al-Shahid and most later scholars (as in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj) relied on Abu Hanifa. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
The operative fatwa is from the later authorities despite early preference for Abu Yusuf.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is: Under Abu Hanifa, ghusl is wajib and fast invalid; under Abu Yusuf, no ghusl and fast valid. Also, if the child is born from a surgical opening, the ruling depends on whether vaginal blood is seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب الغسل عند أبي حنيفة احتياطاً لأن الولادة لا تخلو ظاهراً عن قليل دم، وعند أبي يوسف لا يجب لأنه متعلق بالنفاس ولم يوجد. كذا في فتح القدير وفيه نظر، بل هي نفساء عند أبي حنيفة لما في السراج الوهاج أنه يبطل صومها عند أبي حنيفة إن كانت صائمة، عند أبي يوسف لا غسل عليها ولا يبطل صومها اهـ. فلو لم تكن نفساء لم يبطل صومها، وصحح الشارح الزيلعي قول أبي يوسف معزياً إلى المفيد وقال : لكن يجب عليها الوضوء بخروج النجاسة مع الولد إذ لا تخلو عن رطوبة. وصحح في الفتاوى الظهيرية قول الإمام بالوجوب، وكذا صححه في السراج الوهاج قال : وبه كان يفتي الصدر الشهيد فكان هو المذهب. وفي العناية : وأكثر المشايخ أخذوا بقول أبي حنيفة. وأراد المصنف بالدم الدم الخارج عقب الولادة من الفرج فإنها لو ولدت من قبل سرتها بأن كان ببطنها جرح فانشقت وخرج الولد منها تكون صاحبة جرح سائل لا نفساء وتنقضي به العدة وتصير الأمة أم ولد، ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها وقع لوجود الشرط. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية إلا إذا سال الدم من الأسفل فإنها تصير نفساء ولو ولدت من السرة لأنه وجد خروج الدم من الرحم عقب الولادة. كذا في المحيط.
Then ghusl is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa as a precaution because childbirth apparently does not lack a little blood. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not obligatory because it is related to nifas and it did not occur. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and there is discussion. Rather, she is nufasa' according to Abu Hanifa, because in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj it says that it invalidates her fast according to Abu Hanifa if she was fasting. According to Abu Yusuf, there is no ghusl on her and it does not invalidate her fast. End. So if she were not nufasa', her fast would not be invalidated. And the commentator al-Zayla'i corrected the statement of Abu Yusuf, attributing it to al-Mufid, and said: But she must perform wudu due to the exit of impurity with the child, as it does not lack moisture. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) regarding obligation is corrected, and likewise in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj he said: And with it the Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa, so it is the madhhab. In al-Inaya: Most of the mashayikh took the statement of Abu Hanifa. And the author meant by 'blood' the blood that exits after childbirth from the vagina. So if she gave birth from her navel, meaning there was a wound in her belly that split and the child came out from it, she is a person with a flowing wound, not nufasa', and the waiting period ends with it, and the slave-woman becomes an umm walad. And if her divorce was conditioned on her giving birth, it occurs due to the existence of the condition. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya, unless the blood flows from below, in which case she becomes nufasa' even if she gave birth from the navel, because the exit of blood from the womb after childbirth has occurred. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a woman who gives birth but does not see any blood. The two positions are: (1) Abu Hanifa holds that ghusl is obligatory because childbirth is normally accompanied by at least a little blood, so she is considered nufasa' as a precaution; her fast is invalidated if she was fasting. (2) Abu Yusuf holds that ghusl is not obligatory because it is tied to the existence of nifas blood, and it is absent; her fast remains valid. The author then notes that many later scholars (like al-Zayla'i) preferred Abu Yusuf's view, but the relied-upon fatwa (especially from Sadr al-Shahid) is that of Abu Hanifa. The passage also clarifies that the blood in question is the one exiting from the vagina after childbirth; if the child emerges from the abdomen via a surgical opening, she is not nufasa' unless blood flows from the vagina, indicating uterine origin. The practical consequences include the completion of the waiting period and the status of an umm walad.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): Full-body ritual washing, obligatory after menstruation, nifas, and sexual impurity.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has given birth to her master's child; she is freed upon his death.
نفساء (nufasa'): A woman experiencing postpartum bleeding (nifas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is ghusl obligatory for a woman who gives birth without seeing blood?
A) Because she must have seen some blood
B) As a precaution because childbirth normally involves some blood
C) Because she is in a state of janaba
D) Because of the impurity of the child

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that a woman who gives birth without vaginal blood is considered nufasa' as a precaution, making ghusl wajib and her fast invalid.

Onset of Nifas: Most of the Child Born
States the ruling on blood after most of the child is born and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when nifas begins and distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar.

Core Idea
Nifas begins when most of the child is born, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical practical question: when does postpartum bleeding (nifas) start? It presents the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that blood after the birth of most of the child is nifas, along with the dissenting view of Muhammad and Zufar. The text also provides a compassionate accommodation for the woman to pray without harming her child.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: blood after the exit of most of the child is considered nifas. This means the woman is exempt from prayer.
The word 'أكثر الولد' (most of the child) is key – it refers to the head and most of the body.
2
Identify the opposing view
Contrast with Muhammad and Zufar: they require the entire child to be delivered for nifas to be established. For them, blood before complete delivery does not exempt her from prayer.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on the definition of 'birth'.
3
Note the practical consequence
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: if nifas begins, she does not pray; if not, she must pray and if she omits prayer she is sinful.
This difference affects her religious obligations immediately.
4
Understand the practical accommodation
Examine the ruling for a woman still delivering: she is instructed to use a vessel or dig a pit to prevent blood from soiling the prayer area, so she can pray without harm to the child.
This shows the fiqh's concern for both worship and health.
5
Recognize the authority weight
Note that the text transmits the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from al-Zahiriyya and al-Muhit, indicating it is from the zahir al-riwayah.
The majority of later Hanafi scholars adopt this view as mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدم الخارج عقب خروج أكثر الولد كالخارج عقب كله فيكون نفاساً، وإن خرج الأقل لا يكون حكمها حكم النفساء ولا تسقط عنها الصلاة ولو لم تصل تكون عاصية لربها . ثم كيف تصلي؟ قالوا : يؤتى بقدر فيجعل القدر تحتها أو يحفر لها حفيرة وتجلس هناك وتصلي كيلا تؤذي ولدها . كذا في الظهيرية ونقله في المحيط عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وعند محمد وزفر : إذا خرج أكثره لا يكون نفاساً لأن عندهما النفاس لا يثبت إلا بوضع الحمل كله .
And the blood that exits after the exit of most of the child is like the blood that exits after all of it, so it is nifas. And if only a little exits, her ruling is not the ruling of a nufasa', and prayer is not waived for her, and if she does not pray she is sinful to her Lord. Then how does she pray? They said: A pot is brought and placed under her, or a pit is dug for her and she sits there and prays so as not to harm her child. Thus in al-Zahiriyya, transmitted in al-Muhit from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad and Zufar: If most of it exits, it is not nifas because according to them nifas is only established by the delivery of the entire child.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when nifas begins. It states that blood exiting after the birth of most of the child (i.e., after the head and most of the body) is considered nifas, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. If only a small part exits, the woman is not considered in nifas; she must still pray, and if she does not, she is sinful. For such a woman who is still in the process of delivery, she is instructed to place a vessel under her or sit in a pit to avoid soiling the place of prayer. However, Muhammad and Zufar hold that nifas only begins after the entire child is delivered, because the ruling of nifas is tied to the complete separation of the child. The practical consequence affects whether she must pray and ghusl. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) consider the blood after most of the child as nifas.

Key Terms
نفاس (nifas): In this context, the blood that makes the woman exempt from prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when does nifas begin?
A) When the entire child is born
B) When the child's head appears
C) When most of the child is born
D) When the blood stops

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that postpartum nifas commences with the birth of most of the child, exempting the woman from prayer.

Blood of a Pregnant Woman is Istihadah
States that blood seen during pregnancy is considered istihadah and provides the proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers blood during pregnancy as istihadah and apply the ruling to a pregnant woman who experiences bleeding.

Core Idea
Blood during pregnancy is istihadah because the womb is blocked by the child; it cannot be menstrual.

The text explains a specific Hanafi ruling concerning a pregnant woman who sees blood. While many assume any uterine bleeding is menstruation, the Hanafi school classifies it as istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding) due to the closure of the cervix by the fetus. This ruling has practical consequences for prayer, fasting, and marital waiting periods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
First, note the subject: the blood seen by a pregnant woman. The ruling is that it is classified as istihadah, not hayd (menstruation) or nifas (postpartum bleeding).
This classification determines whether the woman must perform prayers and fasts despite the bleeding.
2
Understand the anatomical reasoning
The text explains that during pregnancy, the mouth of the uterus is blocked by the child, preventing blood from exiting. Therefore, any bleeding that occurs is not from the normal menstrual cycle but from a different cause, making it istihadah.
This is a rational (ta'lili) explanation based on physical obstruction.
3
Analyze the prophetic proof
The proof cited is a prophetic statement (hadith) linking the presence of blood to the emptiness of the womb. In the context of 'iddah (waiting period) and marriage, blood indicates the womb is empty. Conversely, during pregnancy, the absence of blood is expected; thus any blood is abnormal and classified as istihadah.
The hadith is used as evidence that blood is a sign of an empty womb, supporting the conclusion that bleeding during pregnancy cannot be menstrual.
4
Apply the ruling to practice
Since the blood is istihadah, the pregnant woman must continue to pray, fast (if Ramadan), and engage in marital relations as normal. She is not considered in a state of major impurity (janabah) or menstruation.
She must perform wudu for each obligatory prayer, as istihadah does not invalidate wudu after cleaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ودم الحامل استحاضة لانسداد فم الرحم بالولد فلا يخرج منه دم ثم يخرج بخروج الولد للانفتاح به، ولذا حكم الشارع بكون وجود الدم دليلاً على فراغ الرحم في قوله «ألا لا تنكح الحبالى حتى يضعن ولا الحبالى حتى يستبرأن بحيضة.
His saying: And the blood of a pregnant woman is istihadah because the mouth of the womb is blocked by the child, so no blood exits from it; then it exits with the exit of the child due to its opening. Therefore, the Lawgiver ruled that the presence of blood is evidence of the emptiness of the womb, in His saying: 'Indeed, do not marry pregnant women until they deliver, nor pregnant women until they are cleared by a menstruation.'
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that blood seen during pregnancy is istihadah (non-menstrual dysfunctional bleeding), not menstruation or nifas. The reason is that during pregnancy, the mouth of the uterus is closed by the fetus, so no blood can exit. Blood only exits after delivery when the cervix opens. This is supported by the hadith (or statement) that blood indicates the womb is empty, which is used to determine the end of the waiting period and the clearance of the womb before marriage. The practical implication is that a pregnant woman who sees blood must still pray and fast, and it does not affect her state of purity.

Key Terms
الحامل (al-hamil): A pregnant woman.
استحاضة (istihadah): Non-menstrual uterine bleeding that is not part of the normal cycle.

Key Takeaway
Blood seen during pregnancy is classified as istihadah in the Hanafi school, based on uterine blockage and prophetic evidence, meaning the woman must continue her religious duties.

Critical Refinement on Nifas Definition: The Woman Without Blood
Critical refinement of nifas definition and internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of nifas and state the preponderant ruling regarding a woman who gives birth without blood, citing the refined position.

Core Idea
A woman who gives birth without blood is still nufasa' because the birth itself counts as constructive blood.

This passage delves into a subtle but important question within the Hanafi school: if a woman gives birth but sees no blood, is she in a state of post-partum impurity (nifas)? The school has an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Later scholars critically refined the definition of nifas to include constructive (hukmi) blood, affirming that according to the preponderant view, she is indeed nufasa'. This lesson will walk through the arguments and the refinement that settled the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute centers on a woman who delivers a child but experiences no visible blood. Is she considered nufasa' (post-partum) or pure?
2
Understand the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that she is nufasa'. Abu Yusuf initially agreed but later retracted, stating she is pure. The source quotes multiple narrations.
The narration from al-Hasan from Abu Yusuf supports Abu Hanifa but is considered nawadir.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
Al-Zayla'i and Abu Ali al-Daqa'iq argued that the very exit of the child is nifas. This is a strong textual proof, making her nufasa' in reality, not merely by precaution.
This refutes the idea that the ruling is only precautionary.
4
Analyze the critical refinement
Later scholars like al-Qahistani refined the definition: 'Nifas is blood' but include constructive (hukmi) blood. The birth itself, even without visible blood, is treated as constructive blood.
This refinement resolves the inconsistency in the precautionary argument.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The preponderant view (mufta bihi) is that the woman is nufasa'. This is supported by al-Zayla'i, al-Siraj, Sadr al-Shahid, and others.
The tarjih authority is based on later Hanafi masters.

So he said in al-Nahr: It is not necessary that from the invalidation of her fast follows the affirmation of her nifas, because it is possible that it is also a precaution like ghusl. And he made in al-Siraj the cause the same for both, namely precaution. And how could it be accepted that obligating ghusl does not necessitate proof of her nifas, and he did not accept it for fasting? And I do not see a difference between them. Yes, the apparent meaning of the sharh indicates that she is nufasa' according to the Imam. End. Some virtuous persons said: And one can differentiate that ghusl is a means, so it does not necessitate, because it is subsidiary, unlike fasting. Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hishiya) of the earlier discussion on the definition of nifas and the dispute about a woman who gives birth without blood. The author engages with a claim in al-Nahr that invalidating her fast does not prove she is nufasa' because it could be precautionary. He refutes this by noting that the cause for both ghusl and fasting is the same precaution in al-Siraj, and if ghusl is precautionary, why would fasting not also be? He then cites al-Zayla'i's view that the very birth is nifas, implying she is truly nufasa', not just by precaution. He also quotes from al-Nihaya that in one narration from Abu Yusuf and according to Abu Hanifa, she is nufasa', though Abu Yusuf later retracted. Finally, he quotes al-Qahistani that nifas includes both real and constructive blood, which may include this case. The passage clarifies that the preponderant view is that she is indeed nufasa'.

Ghusl for Childbirth Without Blood: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl obligation and nifas status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding nifas without blood and apply the mufta bihi ruling (ghusl wajib) to determine the woman's obligations.

Core Idea
Childbirth itself triggers nifas according to Abu Hanifa, even if no blood is seen – precaution overrides absence of evidence.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: what is the ruling for a woman who gives birth but does not see any blood? The debate centers on whether nifas (postnatal bleeding) is established by the act of birth itself or only by actual blood flow. Abu Hanifa rules that ghusl is wajib as a precaution, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi principles of istihsan and tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and disputed ruling
What is the act? Childbirth without visible blood. What is the disputed hukm? Is ghusl wajib upon her? Is she considered nufasa'?
The core question: does she have the rulings of nifas?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa: She is nufasa' and ghusl is wajib. Basis: istihsan (precaution) because childbirth is normally accompanied by some blood, even if not visible.
Also: her fast is invalidated if she was fasting.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and its basis
Abu Yusuf: No ghusl; she is not nufasa'. Basis: nifas is established only by actual blood flow after birth. Since no blood is seen, it did not occur.
Al-Zayla'i adds that wudu is obligatory due to unavoidable moisture.
4
Note the views of Muhammad and Zufar
They require the full delivery of the child for nifas; if only a majority emerges, it is not nifas according to them.
This affects rulings on the minority partial emergence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position and its tarjih
Later sources (al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, al-Inayah) prefer Abu Hanifa's view. Most mashayikh follow it. The operative ruling for fatwa is that ghusl is wajib.
Tarjih reasons: precaution in ritual purity, fatwa of Sadr al-Shahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصنف أنها لو ولدت ولم تردماً لا تكون نفساء . ثم يجب الغسل عند أبي حنيفة احتياطاً لأن الولادة لا تخلو ظاهراً عن قليل دم، وعند أبي يوسف لا يجب لأنه متعلق بالنفاس ولم يوجد. كذا في فتح القدير وفيه نظر، بل هي نفساء عند أبي حنيفة لما في السراج الوهاج أنه يبطل صومها عند أبي حنيفة إن كانت صائمة، عند أبي يوسف لا غسل عليها ولا يبطل صومها اهـ. فلو لم تكن نفساء لم يبطل صومها، وصحح الشارح الزيلعي قول أبي يوسف معزياً إلى المفيد وقال : لكن يجب عليها الوضوء بخروج النجاسة مع الولد إذ لا تخلو عن رطوبة. وصحح في الفتاوى الظهيرية قول الإمام بالوجوب، وكذا صححه في السراج الوهاج قال : وبه كان يفتي الصدر الشهيد فكان هو المذهب. وفي العناية : وأكثر المشايخ أخذوا بقول أبي حنيفة. وأراد المصنف بالدم الدم الخارج عقب الولادة من الفرج فإنها لو ولدت من قبل سرتها بأن كان ببطنها جرح فانشقت وخرج الولد منها تكون صاحبة جرح سائل لا نفساء وتنقضي به العدة وتصير الأمة أم ولد، ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها وقع لوجود الشرط. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية إلا إذا سال الدم من الأسفل فإنها تصير نفساء ولو ولدت من السرة لأنه وجد خروج الدم من الرحم عقب الولادة. كذا في المحيط . والدم الخارج عقب خروج أكثر الولد كالخارج عقب كله فيكون نفاساً، وإن خرج الأقل لا يكون حكمها حكم النفساء ولا تسقط عنها الصلاة ولو لم تصل تكون عاصية لربها . ثم كيف تصلي؟ قالوا : يؤتى بقدر فيجعل القدر تحتها أو يحفر لها حفيرة وتجلس هناك وتصلي كيلا تؤذي ولدها . كذا في الظهيرية ونقله في المحيط عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وعند محمد وزفر : إذا خرج أكثره لا يكون نفاساً لأن عندهما النفاس لا يثبت إلا بوضع الحمل كله .
The author (al-Musannif) states that if a woman gives birth and does not see blood, she is not nufasa'. Then ghusl becomes obligatory according to Abu Hanifa as a precaution, because childbirth is apparently not free of a little blood. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not obligatory because it is connected to nifas and it (nifas) did not occur. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And there is a consideration: rather, she is nufasa' according to Abu Hanifa because in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj it is mentioned that it invalidates her fast if she was fasting. According to Abu Yusuf, there is no ghusl upon her and her fast is not invalidated. So if she were not nufasa', her fast would not be invalidated. Al-Sharih al-Zayla'i corrected the statement of Abu Yusuf, attributing it to al-Mufid, and said: However, wudu becomes obligatory upon her due to the exit of impurity with the child, for it is not free of moisture. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah, the statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa) on obligation was corrected, and likewise in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, where it says: And the Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa with it, so it is the madhhab. In al-Inayah: And most of the mashayikh adopted the statement of Abu Hanifa. And the author meant by 'blood' the blood that exits after childbirth from the vagina; for if she gives birth from her navel, for example there is a wound in her belly that splits and the child comes out from there, she is a person with a flowing wound, not nufasa', and the 'idda is completed by it and the slave woman becomes umm walad. And if her divorce was suspended upon her giving birth, it occurs due to the existence of the condition. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah, unless blood flows from below, then she becomes nufasa', even if she gave birth from the navel, because the exit of blood from the womb after childbirth occurred. Thus in al-Muhit. And the blood that exits after the majority of the child has emerged is like the blood that exits after the whole, so it is nifas. And if the lesser part exits, her ruling is not the ruling of a nufasa', and salah is not dropped from her; and if she does not pray, she is sinful towards her Lord. Then how does she pray? They said: A pot is brought and placed under her, or a pit is dug for her, and she sits there and prays so as not to harm her child. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah, and it is narrated in al-Muhit from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad and Zufar: If the majority has exited, it is not nifas, because according to them, nifas is not established except by delivering the whole child.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a woman who gives birth but does not see any blood. The core issue is whether she is considered nufasa' (in postnatal bleeding) and whether ghusl is obligatory upon her. Abu Hanifa holds that ghusl is obligatory as a precautionary measure (istihsan), because childbirth is generally not devoid of some blood (even if not visible externally). Therefore, she is effectively nufasa' for certain rulings: her fast is invalidated if she was fasting. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, says no ghusl is required because nifas is defined by the actual flow of blood after birth, which she did not experience. Al-Zayla'i upholds Abu Yusuf's view but adds that wudu is obligatory due to the unavoidable moisture/impurity accompanying the child. Later sources like al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj prefer Abu Hanifa's ruling, and most mashayikh follow Abu Hanifa. The passage also clarifies the definition: blood here means blood exiting from the vagina after birth. If the child emerges from the navel (cesarean), she is not nufasa' unless blood flows from the lower area. The ruling on blood after the majority of the child has emerged: it is nifas; if after the lesser part, it is not, and she must pray (described with a pot or pit to protect the child). Muhammad and Zufar disagree, requiring full delivery for nifas. The practical outcome affects ghusl, fasting, and prayer obligations.

Key Terms
نفاس (nifās): Postnatal bleeding that occurs after childbirth; the woman is called nufasa'.
نفساء (nufasā'): A woman in the state of postnatal bleeding.
غسل (ghusl): Ritual bath (full ablution) required after cessation of nifas or for other states of major impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She is nufasa' and ghusl is wajib as a precaution.
C) She must only perform wudu.
D) She must perform ghusl only if she fasted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, childbirth triggers nifas even without visible blood, making ghusl wajib as a precautionary measure (mufta bihi).

The Legal Definition of Saqt (Miscarriage) and Its Rulings
Definition and legal consequences of miscarriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of saqt, identify the condition for it to be considered a child (walad), and apply its consequences for nifas, umm walad, and hanth.

Core Idea
A miscarried fetus is a child only when some limb formation is apparent – this usually occurs after 120 days.

This passage addresses a critical question: when does a miscarriage legally count as a birth? The answer determines whether the woman experiences nifas (postnatal bleeding), whether a slave woman becomes an umm walad, and whether an oath is broken. The defining criterion is the visibility of some created limbs or features, which typically occurs after 120 days. We will parse the definition and its implications, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definition of 'saqt' and understand its linguistic meaning ('the child that falls before completion') and legal application. Note that the definition is tied to the appearance of some creation.
2
Extract the condition for child status
The key condition is 'an yazhara ba'du khalqihi' (that some of its creation is apparent). This is the decisive factor: if no limbs or features are visible, it is not a child legally.
The condition is restricted to avoid treating unformed tissue as a birth.
3
Apply the ruling to three consequences
The passage lists three results: (1) the woman becomes nufasa' (in nifas), (2) a slave woman becomes umm walad if her master claims the child, (3) a man becomes hanith (breaks an oath) if he swore on childbirth. All depend on the condition being met.
4
Understand the time estimate
The passage states that creation does not become apparent until 120 days, citing Shaykh al-Zayla'i. This aligns with the hadith of the stages of creation: 40 days nutfah, 40 days 'alaqah, 40 days mudghah, then the soul is blown.
Before 120 days, the fetus is not considered a human life; treatment to induce miscarriage is permissible.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage
The passage includes a report from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf: if a woman marries and gives birth to a formed child within less than four months and ten days, Muhammad says the marriage is invalid, Abu Yusuf says it is valid because she could have been pregnant before. This illustrates the practical application of the 120-day rule.
The dispute shows the importance of the formation criterion in establishing lineage and marriage validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

على ذلك. وإن أسقطت بعد أيامها فإنها تصلي من ذلك الوقت قدر عادتها في الطهر بالشك، ثم تترك قدر عادتها في الحيض المتخلل في مدته ونفاس من ولدت ولم تردماً وهذا قول أبي حنيفة .اهـ. وبه يحصل الجواب عما تمسك به صاحب فتح القدير قوله : (ولا يستبين خلقه إلا في مائة وعشرين يوماً الخ) قال في النهر أقول : إنما ذكر الشارح هذا في نكاح الرقيق وكون المراد به ما ذكر ممنوع فقد وجه في البدائع وغيرها ذلك بأنه يكون أربعين يوماً نطفة وأربعين علقة وأربعين مضغة وعبارته في عقد الفرائد قالوا: يباح لها أن تعالج في استنزال الدم ما دام الحمل مضغة أو علقة ولم يخلق له عضو، وقدروا تلك المدة بمائة وعشرين يوماً، وإنما أباحوا ذلك لأنه ليس بآدمي .اهـ. ولا مانع أنه بعد هذه المدة تخلق أعضاؤه وتنفخ فيه الروح .اهـ. ويدل على ما قاله ما في شرح الوهبانية لابن الشحنة عن ستة عن هشام عن محمد: تزوّج امرأة لم يكن قبله لها زوج وبنى بها فجاءت بولد لأقل من النكاح فالنكاح فاسد عندي، وعند أبي يوسف لأنه تزوّجها وهي حامل وإن جاءت به وقد استبان بعض خلقه لأكثر من أربعة أشهر وعشر فالنكاح جائز، وإن جاءت به لأقل ففاسد اهـ. وهذا لأن تزوجها وهي حامل لأن الخلق لا يستبين إلا في مائة وعشرين يوماً، وزيادة العشرة التي مدة الحيض لاحتمال مقارنة النكاح للحيض ثم قال : والذي يفهم من ذلك أن استبانة بعض الخلق لا تكون أقل من أربعة أشهر ولهذا قال في الواقعات: لو جاءت به الأربعة أشهر إلا يوماً كان من .
His saying: 'And the saqt (miscarriage) such that some of its creation is apparent is a child. Saqt with kasr and tathlith is a language term, as in al-Misbah. It is a child that falls before its completion, like the one that falls after completion in rulings regarding the 'idda. So the woman becomes nufasa' by it, and the slave woman becomes umm walad through it if the master claims it, and he becomes hanith (breaking an oath) by it if he suspended his oath on giving birth. And its creation does not become apparent except in one hundred and twenty days, as mentioned by al-Sharih al-Zayla'i in the chapter on establishing lineage. And what is meant is the blowing of the soul; otherwise, what is observed is the appearance of its creation before that. He restricted with 'if it appears' because if nothing of its creation appears, it is not a child and these rulings are not established, so there is no nifas for her. However, if it is possible to make the visible blood hayd, by it continuing to the minimum duration of hayd and preceded by a complete purity, it is made hayd; and if not possible, it is istihadah. Thus in al-Inayah. And if she does not know whether it was formed or not, for example she expelled it from the outlet and blood continued: if she miscarried at the beginning of her days (of her habit), she leaves prayer for the duration of her habit with certainty because she is either ha'id or nufasa'. Then she performs ghusl and prays for the duration of her habit in purity with doubt, due to the possibility of her being nufasa' or tahirah. Then she leaves prayer for the duration of her habit with certainty because she is either nufasa' or ha'id. Then she performs ghusl and prays for the duration of her habit in purity with certainty if she has completed forty days from the time of miscarriage; otherwise with doubt in the amount that falls within it, and with certainty in the remainder. Then she continues upon that. And if she miscarried after her days (of habit), she prays from that time for the duration of her habit in purity with doubt, then leaves for the duration of her habit in the hayd that intervenes in its period. And nifas of a woman who gave birth but did not see blood: this is the statement of Abu Hanifa. With it is the answer to what the author of Fath al-Qadir clung to. His saying: 'And its creation does not become apparent except in one hundred and twenty days...' He said in al-Nahr: I say: The commentator only mentioned this in the chapter on marriage of slaves, and the claim that what is meant is what was mentioned is disputed. For it was explained in al-Bada'i' and others that it is forty days a nutfah, forty days a 'alaqah, and forty days a mudghah. And his expression in 'Uqdat al-Fara'id: They said: It is permissible for her to treat to bring down blood as long as the pregnancy is mudghah or 'alaqah and no limb has been created, and they estimated that period as one hundred and twenty days. They only permitted it because it is not a human. So there is no objection that after this period its limbs are created and the soul is blown into it. And evidence for what he said is what is in Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah by Ibn al-Shihnah on the authority of Sittah from Hisham from Muhammad: A man married a woman who had no prior husband and consummated, then she brought a child at less than (the duration of) marriage, so the marriage is invalid according to me (Muhammad), and according to Abu Yusuf because he married her while she was pregnant. And if she brings it and some of its creation has become apparent at more than four months and ten days, the marriage is valid, and if less, it is invalid. This is because he married her while she was pregnant, as creation does not become apparent except in one hundred and twenty days, and the extra ten days that are the duration of hayd (menstruation) are due to the possibility of the marriage coinciding with menstruation. Then he said: And what is understood from this is that the appearance of some creation is not less than four months, and for this reason it is said in al-Waqi'at: If she brought it at four months less a day, it is from...
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-ruling passage about saqt (miscarriage/fetus that is expelled before full term). It defines the term saqt linguistically and legally. The key condition: a saqt is considered a 'child' (walad) for legal purposes only if some of its creation is apparent (zahara ba'du khalqihi), meaning that limbs or features are recognizable. This typically occurs after 120 days (four months) of gestation, which is when the soul is believed to be blown into the fetus according to hadith. If the miscarried matter shows no distinct creation, it is not a child, and the woman does not become nufasa'. Instead, the blood may be considered hayd if it meets the conditions (minimum duration and preceded by a complete purity), otherwise it is istihadah. The passage also provides a detailed schedule for a woman who miscarries and is uncertain whether the fetus was formed—she follows her established habit of hayd and nifas with a combination of certainty and doubt to determine when to pray and when to abstain. The nifas of a woman who gives birth but sees no blood is attributed to Abu Hanifa's view earlier. The author then refutes the interpretation of Fath al-Qadir regarding the 120 days by citing various Hanafi sources that confirm the 120-day period for limb formation, after which it is considered a human life; before that, treatment to induce miscarriage is permissible because it is not yet a human. A case from the Wahbaniyyah illustrates the application: if a woman gives birth to a child with apparent limbs within less than four months and ten days from marriage, the marriage is invalid because she must have been pregnant before marriage (zina). This all ties into the broader Hanafi rulings on nifas, 'idda, and lineage.

Key Terms
سقط (saqṭ): A miscarried fetus that is expelled before full term. Legally, it is considered a child (walad) only if some of its creation is apparent.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.
حِنث (ḥinth): Breaking an oath; if a man swears an oath related to childbirth, the appearance of a formed child constitutes hanth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum period after which a miscarried fetus is considered a 'child' for rulings of nifas?
A) 60 days
B) 90 days
C) 120 days
D) 180 days

Key Takeaway
The legal status of a miscarriage as a birth hinges on the visibility of some created limbs, established after 120 days; without it, no nifas, umm walad, or hanth.

When Does a Miscarried Fetus Trigger Nifas?
Expands the definition of sibt for nifas rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a miscarried fetus (sibt) triggers nifas and apply the rulings to scenarios of uncertainty about the fetus's formation.

Core Idea
A miscarried fetus triggers nifas only if even a single limb appears; otherwise the blood may be hayd or istihadah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the definition of nifas from a sibt (miscarried fetus). The Hanafi school holds that a sibt is considered a child for nifas rulings if any part of its creation is apparent. This lesson unpacks the condition, the legal consequences, and how to handle uncertainty when the formation is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Find the condition in the text: the sibt must show some creation (e.g., a finger). If it does, it is legally a child.
The text says 'an ẓahara baʿḍu khalqihi' — 'if some of its creation appears'.
2
State the Ruling When Condition Met
If the condition is met, the woman becomes nufasa' (postpartum bleeding), and the blood is nifas. She is excused from prayer and fasting during nifas.
Maximum nifas is 40 days; excess is istihadah.
3
State the Ruling When Condition Not Met
If no creation appears, the blood is not nifas. It may be classified as hayd if it meets the conditions (e.g., lasts at least 3 days and preceded by a complete purity), otherwise it is istihadah.
If hayd, she leaves prayer; if istihadah, she must pray after wudu.
4
Understand the Expansion: Uncertainty Scenario
The text details a case where the woman does not know if the fetus was formed. She must follow a pattern of prayer/leave based on certainty and doubt, as per Abu Hanifah.
The pattern involves alternating between certainty of being nufasa'/ha'id and doubt.
5
Apply the 120-Day Rule
The creation becomes apparent only after 120 days, which corresponds to the blowing of the spirit. This affects rulings on lineage and umm walad.
The text cites al-Zayla'i and others on this point.

Book of Purity - Chapter of Menstruation. If some of its creation appears, it is a child, and there is no limit for its minimum, and its maximum is forty days, and what exceeds is istihadah. And postpartum bleeding of twins is from the first. And it... She becomes... For this reason it is said in al-Waqi'at: If she brought it at four months less one day, it would be from... al-Muntaqa min akthar. Certainly. And the sum of all this is that there is no ruling for doubt and caution is obligatory, and in many copies of al-Khulasah there is a mistake in the depiction here from the copyists, so be cautious of it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Nihayah: If she sees blood before the miscarriage of the sibt and sees blood after it, then if it was distinct in creation, then what she saw before it is not hayd and she is nufasa' in what she saw after it; and if it is not distinct in creation, then what she saw after it is hayd if possible as we have presented.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the ruling on nifas from a miscarried fetus (sibt). It begins by stating the general rule: if some part of the sibt's creation appears, it is considered a child, and the woman becomes nufasa'. The minimum duration of nifas is not fixed (the text will discuss this later), and the maximum is forty days; beyond that is istihadah. For twins, nifas is counted from the first delivery. The commentator then explains that blood seen before the delivery of most of the child is considered istihadah, so the woman should perform wudu if able, or tayammum, and gesture in prayer; she should not delay. This is because she is like one who has an excuse (like a healthy person who is unable to use water). The passage then defines the term 'sibt' (miscarried fetus) linguistically and legally. Legally, if the sibt shows any sign of creation (e.g., a limb), it is treated as a child for purposes of nifas, umm walad, oaths, and lineage. The creation becomes apparent only after 120 days, which corresponds to the blowing of the spirit. However, if no creation is apparent, the blood may be classified as hayd (if it meets the conditions) or istihadah. The text then details a scenario where the woman does not know if the sibt was formed: she must act with caution, following a pattern of prayer and fasting based on certainty and doubt. This is the position of Abu Hanifah. The commentary then addresses a possible objection from Fath al-Qadir regarding the 120-day period, citing various sources that support the Hanafi view. Finally, a summary rule from al-Nihayah is given: blood before a formed sibt is not hayd, and blood after it is nifas; if the sibt is not formed, blood after it is hayd if possible.

Key Terms
السقط (al-siqtu): The miscarried fetus before its full development; if some creation appears, it is legally a child.
نفاس (nifās): Postpartum bleeding following childbirth or a formed sibt.
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Irregular vaginal bleeding that is not hayd or nifas; requires wudu for each prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman miscarries and sees blood. The fetus has a visible finger. According to the Hanafi school, what is the correct ruling?
A) The blood is not nifas because the fetus was not fully formed.
B) The blood is nifas because some creation appeared, and she is excused from prayer.
C) The blood is hayd because it is less than 40 days.
D) The blood is istihadah and she must pray.

Key Takeaway
A miscarried fetus triggers nifas only if some part of its creation appears; otherwise the blood may be hayd or istihadah based on its characteristics.

Taharah — Purification

Duration of Nifas: Minimum and Maximum
Ruling statement with explanatory expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that nifas has no minimum for ibadah and a maximum of forty days, and distinguish when the minimum matters for iddah.

Core Idea
For prayer and fasting, nifas has no minimum; its maximum is forty days.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays down the foundational rule: postpartum bleeding (nifas) has no set minimum duration for the purposes of prayer and fasting, but its maximum is consistently held to be forty days. Beyond forty days, any blood is considered istihadah (non-menstrual irregular bleeding). However, when it comes to calculating the 'iddah (waiting period) of a divorced woman, the minimum duration of nifas becomes crucial, and the Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This lesson focuses on the general ruling; the dispute for iddah is covered in a separate lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling in the matn
Read the first few lines of the passage: 'ولا حد لأقله وأكثره أربعون يوماً' (no limit for its minimum, its maximum is forty days). This is the core ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
This statement applies to the status of blood for acts of worship.
2
Distinguish between ibadah and iddah contexts
Note that the commentator explicitly says 'لا حد له يعني في حق الصلاة والصوم' (no limit for it meaning regarding prayer and fasting). Then he introduces a second context: 'أما إذا كان احتيج إليه لانقضاء العدة' (as for when it is needed for the completion of iddah). Recognize that the minimum is only defined in iddah.
The same blood has different legal minima depending on the purpose.
3
Understand the maximum limit
The text states 'وأكثره أربعون يوماً والزائد استحاضة' (its maximum is forty days, and what exceeds is istihadah). This is the agreed-upon position in the Hanafi school, supported by narrations from the Companions.
This is the basis for all Hanafi fatwas on nifas.
4
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a woman who gives birth and sees blood for only a few hours. For prayer and fasting, she is considered in nifas only while blood is flowing; when it stops, she must pray and fast. If the blood returns within forty days, the intervening purity does not separate the nifas period – the entire forty days are considered nifas for the purpose of prohibition of prayer and fasting.
This is based on Abu Hanifah's principle that a tuhr (pure period) within the forty days does not break the continuity of nifas.
5
Contrast with the dispute for iddah
Recognize that while the above is clear for worship, the passage then introduces a major dispute for iddah calculation. This is a separate issue that will be explored in the next lesson.
The dispute among Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad only pertains to iddah, not to the basic rules of prayer and fasting.

His statement: (And there is no limit for its minimum, i.e., nifas, because the arrival of the child indicates the exit from the womb, so it suffices without its extension by what is made a sign for it, unlike hayd. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in his Mabsut: Our companions agreed that the minimum of nifas is what exists; for when she gives birth, if she sees blood for an hour then the blood stops, she fasts and prays and what she saw was nifas. There is no disagreement among our companions on this. The disagreement is only when the minimum of nifas must be considered in the completion of the iddah, such as if he said to her: 'When you give birth, you are divorced,' and she said: 'My iddah has ended,' what amount is considered for the minimum of nifas along with three hayds? According to Abu Hanifah, its minimum is considered twenty-five days; according to Abu Yusuf, eleven; and according to Muhammad, an hour. As for regarding fasting and prayer, its minimum is what exists. Thus in al-Nihayah.
